ويرد القلب في القرآن بمعنى من المعاني الثلاثة التالية: أولها: العقل وذلك في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَى لِمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ قَلْب﴾ [ق: ٣٧/٥٠]. الثاني: بمعنى الرأي والتدبير، وذلك في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿تَحْسَبُهُمْ جَمِيعاً وَقُلُوبُهُمْ شَتّى﴾ [الحشر: ١٤/٥٩]. الثالث: بمعنى حقيقة القلب الذي في الصدر، وذلك في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنْ تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُور﴾ [الحج: ٤٦/٢٢]. وهذا المعنى الثالث إنما يشير إلى الجانب الوظيفي للعقل. فكما أن البصر اسم للإدراك التام الكامل الحاصل بالعين التي في الرأس؛ فالبصيرة اسم للإدراك التام الحاصل في القلب. قال تعالى: ﴿بَلِ الإِنْسَانُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بَصِيرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: ١٤/٧٥]، أي له بنفسه معرفة تامة. أي أن العقل يستخدم في القرآن باعتباره موضعاً للعلم والمعرفة. أَفَلا يَتَدَبَّرُونَ الْقُرْآنَ أَمْ عَلَى قُلُوبٍ أَقْفَالُهَا [محمد: ٢٤/٤٧]. ووفق هذا المعنى فإن القلب في نظر القرآن أداة من أدوات المعرفة، حيث يعتمد القرآن على مخاطبة القلب في معظم رسالته. ومثل هذا الاستنتاج مما قد يُشكل استيعابه بالنظر إلى الشائع من كون العقل وظيفته الإرادة في حين أن القلب وظيفته العاطفة ولكن اهتمام القرآن الواضح بالقلب إلى حد احتوائه ١٢٢ آيةً تتحدث عنه بجوانبه المختلفة، بحيث تتعـدد صوره، فتارة نشعر أننا أمام صورة العقل ذاته، وتارة نشعر أننا أمام العاطفة والمشاعر الوجدانية، وثالثة نشعر أننا أمام الجانبين معاً: جانب العقل وجانب العاطفة وهكذا. ذلك كله مما يساعد على إزالة ذلك الإشكال المفترض. ونتيجة لذكر القلب في القرآن بوصفه اسم جنس مفرداً ومجموعاً، بخلاف العقل الذي ورد مفرداً مشتقاً من اسم جنسه، فإن ذلك دفع ببعض الباحثين لاستخلاص أن ليس هناك شئ مجسّم في جسم الإنسان، لذات اسمه العقل. أما القلب فإن هناك شيئاً لذات اسمه القلب وهو تلك المضغة القائمة في الصدر. الأمر الذي يدعونا للفصل التام بين كل من العقل والقلب، لنصل إلى أن القلب من الألفاظ المشتركة في معانيها، وأن من جملة تلك المعاني (العقل).
The heart occurs in the Quran with one of the following three meanings: First: intellect, as in His statement, the Most High: 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' [Qaf 50:37]. Second: meaning opinion and management, as in His statement, the Most High: 'You think they are united, but their hearts are diverse' [al-Hashr 59:14]. Third: meaning the reality of the heart that is in the chest, as in His statement, the Most High: 'But blind are the hearts that are in the chests' [al-Hajj 22:46]. This third meaning refers to the functional aspect of the intellect. For just as sight is the name for complete and perfect perception obtained by the eye in the head, so insight (basira) is the name for complete perception obtained in the heart. He, the Most High, said: 'Rather, man against himself is insight' [al-Qiyama 75:14], meaning he has complete knowledge of himself. That is, the intellect is used in the Quran as a locus of knowledge and cognition. 'Then do they not reflect upon the Quran, or are there locks upon their hearts?' [Muhammad 47:24]. According to this meaning, the heart in the view of the Quran is an instrument of knowledge, as the Quran relies on addressing the heart in most of its message. This conclusion may be difficult to comprehend given the common notion that the intellect's function is will while the heart's function is emotion, but the Quran's clear emphasis on the heart to the extent that it contains 122 verses speaking about its various aspects, such that its images multiply: sometimes we feel we are facing the image of the intellect itself, sometimes we feel we are facing emotion and sentimental feelings, and a third time we feel we are facing both aspects together: the aspect of intellect and the aspect of emotion, and so on. All of this helps remove that supposed difficulty. As a result of mentioning the heart in the Quran as a generic noun, singular and plural, unlike the intellect which appeared singularly derived from its generic noun, this led some researchers to conclude that there is no bodily thing in the human body with the essence named intellect. As for the heart, there is something with the essence named the heart, which is that piece of flesh located in the chest. This necessitates a complete separation between the intellect and the heart, leading us to the conclusion that the heart is among the polysemous words in its meanings, and that among those meanings is (the intellect).
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage classifies the Quranic meanings of 'qalb' (heart) and relates it to intellect ('aql) as an epistemological tool. The author begins by listing three distinct meanings: (1) intellect itself, citing Quran 50:37 where having a heart implies understanding; (2) opinion and management, as in Quran 59:14 where hearts are described as scattered; (3) the physical organ in the chest, from Quran 22:46 which speaks of blindness of hearts. The third meaning is then explained functionally: just as physical sight (basar) is perception via the eye, insight (basira) is perception via the heart, citing Quran 75:14. Thus the intellect in the Quran is a locus of knowledge, as confirmed by Quran 47:24 questioning whether hearts are locked. The author addresses a common misconception—that intellect is about will and heart about emotion—by showing that the Quran's extensive use of 'heart' (122 verses) encompasses intellect, emotion, and both together. Finally, the author notes that linguistically 'aql appears only as a singular derivative, while 'qalb' appears as a generic noun in singular and plural, leading some researchers to conclude that 'aql has no physical counterpart whereas 'qalb does (the flesh in the chest). Therefore 'qalb' is a homonym including the meaning of intellect, but the physical heart is something else. This analysis uses purely Quranic evidence and traditional lexicography, grounding epistemology in revelation.

The Rational Contradiction Argument for Tawḥīd
Author explains rational impossibility of two gods using Quran and logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the rational contradiction argument against multiple gods and identify its conditional (modus tollens) form.

Core Idea
Rational contradiction is the Quranic proof that only one God can exist.

This passage introduces the method of rational contradiction (al-tamānuʿ al-ʿAqlī) as a logical proof for the oneness of God. The author shows that the intellect necessarily rejects the possibility of two gods because it would lead to unavoidable conflict and cosmic corruption. The reasoning is presented in a conditional form, supported by two Quranic verses that explicitly articulate the consequence of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall logical claim
The passage states that the intellect cannot accept two contradictory statements. The concrete example is that we cannot rationally believe in two gods because that would cause the universe to be unstable.
Focus on the phrase 'امتناع تصديق العقل بقبول المتناقضين'.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The argument can be written as: If there were multiple gods, then disorder and corruption would result (because they would have conflicting wills). This is the antecedent and consequent.
The author mentions 'استحالة استقامة الكون' as the impossibility of the universe being ordered.
3
Find the second premise (via Quran)
The Quranic verses state: 'If there had been in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted.' This confirms the conditional. The verses also imply that the universe is actually orderly (not corrupted).
The verses provide the factual observation that the universe is not ruined.
4
Determine the logical form and conclusion
The argument follows modus tollens: If multiple gods (P), then corruption (Q). But not corruption (¬Q). Therefore, not multiple gods (¬P). This is a valid conditional argument.
This is an implicit modus tollens; the passage does not spell out the second premise explicitly but relies on the reader's knowledge that the universe is ordered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود بهذا الأسلوب امتناع تصديق العقل بقبول المتناقضين لامتناع ذلك بالاستبعاد العقلي. ومن ذلك استحالة أن يقبل العقل بوجود إلهين لاستحالة استقامة الكون، بوصفهما ندين متنازعين في إرادتيهما والتوجيهات الصادرة عن كل واحد منهما في تسيير شؤون الكون والإنسان. وإلى هذا المعنى تشير الآيتان التاليتان: ﴿مَا اتَّخَذَ اللَّهُ مِنْ وَلَدٍ وَمَا كَانَ مَعَهُ مِنْ إِلَهِ إِذًا لَذَهَبَ كُلُّ إِلَهِ بِمَا خَلَقَ وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى بَعْضٍ﴾ [المؤمنون: 91] ﴿أَمِ اتَّخَذُوا آلِهَةً مِنَ الْأَرْضِ هُمْ يُنْشِرُونَ، لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا﴾ [الأنبياء: 21-22]. وَأُسْلُوبُ التَّمَانُعِ الْعَقْلِيِّ مُعْتَبَرٌ فِي تَدْرِيسِ الْعَقِيدَةِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الْخُصُوصِ.
And what is intended by this method is the impossibility of the intellect accepting two contradictories because of its impossibility by rational exclusion. Among that is the impossibility that the intellect accepts the existence of two gods due to the impossibility of the stability of the universe, as they would be two rivals in their wills and directives issuing from each of them in managing the affairs of the universe and humanity. And this meaning is indicated by the two following verses: 'Allah has not taken any son, nor was there any god with Him; then each god would have taken away what he created, and some of them would have risen over others' [al-Mu'minun 91]. 'Or have they taken gods from the earth who resurrect? If there had been in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' [al-Anbiya 21-22]. And the method of rational contradiction is considered especially in teaching creed.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the Quranic method of rational contradiction (al-tamānuʿ al-ʿAqlī). The author explains that the intellect necessarily rejects the simultaneous acceptance of two contradictory statements. As an example, it is rationally impossible to believe in two gods because that would lead to conflict and corruption of the universe. The author then cites two Quranic verses: the first from Surah al-Mu'minun argues that if there were multiple gods, each would take control of its creation and some would dominate others. The second from Surah al-Anbiya states that if there were gods besides Allah, the heavens and earth would be ruined. This logical argument is foundational in teaching Islamic creed (ʿAqīda). The argument follows a conditional form: if multiple gods existed, then the universe would be corrupted; but the universe is not corrupted; therefore, there are not multiple gods.

المتناقضين

Key Takeaway
The rational contradiction argument is a key logical proof for the oneness of God, rooted in Quranic reasoning.

The Qur'anic Call to Intellectual Excellence
Author argues for intellectual education from Qur'anic verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Qur'anic basis for prioritizing intellectual education and explain its implications for Islamic pedagogy.

Core Idea
The Qur'an honors people of understanding; Islamic education must cultivate the intellect.

The author begins with Qur'an 39:18, linking true guidance to following the best of speech. From this, he argues that educational policies should honor intellectual excellence. This lesson unpacks that foundational claim and its implications for curriculum design.

Guided Path
1
Locate the verse and key phrase
Find verse 39:18 in the Arabic passage. Note the phrase 'ulu al-albab' (people of understanding).
2
Extract the educational imperative
The author states that this verse 'certainly indicates' that educational policies should honor intellectual superiority. Identify how he moves from verse to prescription.
He uses the verse to argue for prioritizing mental education in curricula.
3
Identify the logical connection
Because the Qur'an praises understanding, the educational system should foster it. This is a teleological argument from the goal of revelation.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
Think how this principle is applied in fields like usul al-fiqh or kalam, where students are trained to reason through ijtihad.
Without this, education becomes mere memorization.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
الَّذِينَ يَسْتَمِعُونَ الْقَوْلَ فَيَتَّبِعُونَ أَحْسَنَهُ أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ هَدَاهُمُ اللهُ وَأُولَئِكَ هُمْ أُولُو الأَلْباب ﴾ [الزمر: ١٨/٣٩]. وهذا يدلّ بالتأكيد على أن من المفترض أن تتوجّه السياسات التعليمية المنبثقة عن التربية الإسلامية إلى تكريم ذوي التفوق العقلي من المتعلمين، وأن تولي المناهج التعليمية اهتماماً أكبر بجوانب التربية العقلية، كما ينبغي أن تتاح فرص لتقديم الأنشطة ذات الطابع العقلي من ثقافية وفكرية وسواهما، وأن تُقدّم الحوافز الدافعة لدعم ذلك التفاعل، وتلك الأنشطة.
طبيعة عمل العقل
إذا كان العقل على ذلك النحو من الأهمية فما طبيعة عمله؟ بمعنى في أي عالم يقوم العقل بوظائفه : أفي عالم الغيب أم الشهادة؟ وكيف يتجسد دوره في الوحي؟ وكيف يتجسد دور الوحي فيه؟ وحتى يمكننا تحديد الإجابة عن هذين السؤالين يحسن بنا أن ندرك ابتداءً أن العقل ملكة واسعة تجعله يقف وراء الحواس، باعتباره العنصر الحقيقي في عملية الإدراك أو المعرفة العقلية والعلمية، ولذلك فإن دوره المعرفي يتعدى عالم الشهادة إلى عالم الغيب، سواء كان ذلك عن طريق عالم الشهادة نفسه، أم كان عن طريق عالم الغيب في الاستدلال على الإيمان بالله تعالى (۱) - على سبيل المثال - ولكن رُبَّ قائل يعترض على هذا في ضوء ما تم تقريره عند الحديث عن ميدان المعرفة في عالم الغيب، وأن طريقها ليس العقل، كما أنه ليس الحواس، بل الوحي سبيل معرفتها الوحيد. وهنا نرى ضرورة التذكير بما سبقت الإشارة إليه عند الحديث عن ميدان المعرفة في عالم الغيب أيضاً من أن ثمة دلائل تشهد لهذا الإيمان سواء تلك التي تأتي نتائج تلقائية للإيمان بالله تعالى أو تلك التي تبرز في ميداني الآفاق والأنفس من عالم الشهادة، وهي تمثل جزءاً من المشهد الطويل الذي تتم بعض فصوله في عالم الشهادة، وتبقى فصوله الأخرى مطوية في إطار عالم الغيب لأن معرفتنا بها لا تتعدى الخطوط العريضة لعالم الغيب، أما بقية التفصيلات فإن العقل لا يتعدى حدوده إزاءها، إذ إن مصدر ذلك وسبيل معرفته الوحي غير. لا ولكن المشكلة تعود - في جذورها - هنا إلى أصل ذلك النزاع الذي دارت رحاه بين الحسيين والعقليين. فالمشكلة - لدى الفيلسوف مثلاً - هي أن الرجل العادي يؤمن بالمحسوسات على أنها حقائق في ذاتها، بينما يريد الفيلسوف التأكد بالبراهين العقلية من صحة هذا الزعم؟ ذلك - في الواقع جوهر النزاع في نظرية المعرفة بين عنصري العقل البرهاني والاختبار الحسي). ومن قبيل الاعتساف أن يعتقد الفيلسوف أنه وحده المعني بالخطاب العقلي في القرآن دون سواه، ذلك لأن خطاب القرآن للكافة، لأنه يعتمد على البداهة العامة، والحس المشترك (الفطرة)، في حين ترتاب الفلسفة في البداهة العامة ارتياباً كبيراً، وتحاول دحض هذه الفكرة في كثير من الأحيان(٢). إن العقل إذ يؤدي أدواره فإنه يؤديها في وظيفتين رئيستين هما: الأولى: ينظم الإدراك الحسي فيحوله إلى معرفة وتسمى عند العقليين معرفـة بَعْدِيَّة) أي بعد الخبرة والتجربة. الثانية: حين يكتشف العقل المعرفة في استقلال عن الحواس، فتسمى هذه المعرفة (قبليَّة) أي قبل التجربة، أي إنها تقوم على مفهوم الأفكار الأولية، أو النظرية، أو القبلية، حيث تعد نابعة من العقل ذاته باعتباره مصدراً للمعرفة(٣).
Chapter Three: Section Two - The Intellect "Those who listen to the word and follow the best of it; those are the ones whom Allah has guided, and those are the people of understanding" [Az-Zumar 39:18]. This certainly indicates that educational policies emanating from Islamic education should honor those with intellectual superiority among learners, and that curricula should pay greater attention to aspects of intellectual education, and opportunities should be provided for presenting intellectual activities of a cultural and intellectual nature and others, and incentives should be offered to support that interaction and those activities. The Nature of the Intellect's Work: If the intellect is of such importance, what is the nature of its work? That is, in which world does the intellect perform its functions: in the world of the unseen or the visible? And how is its role embodied in revelation? And how is the role of revelation embodied in it? So that we can determine the answer to these two questions, it is good for us to realize initially that the intellect is a vast faculty that makes it stand behind the senses, as it is the real element in the process of perception or intellectual and scientific knowledge. Therefore, its cognitive role extends beyond the visible world to the unseen world, whether that is through the visible world itself, or through the unseen world in inferring faith in Allah the Exalted (1) - for example. But perhaps someone may object to this in light of what has been settled in the discussion about the field of knowledge in the unseen world, and that its path is not the intellect, nor the senses, but revelation is its only way of knowing. Here we see the necessity of recalling what was previously referred to in the discussion about the field of knowledge in the unseen world as well: that there are evidences that testify to this faith, whether those that come as spontaneous results of faith in Allah the Exalted or those that appear in the fields of horizons and souls from the visible world. They represent part of the long scene, some of whose chapters take place in the visible world, while its other chapters remain folded within the framework of the unseen world because our knowledge of them does not go beyond the broad outlines of the unseen world. As for the rest of the details, the intellect does not go beyond its limits regarding them, for the source of that and the path to knowing it is revelation, not the intellect. But the problem - in its roots - here goes back to the origin of that dispute that raged between empiricists and rationalists. The problem - for the philosopher, for example - is that the common person believes in sensory objects as truths in themselves, while the philosopher wants to verify with rational proofs the validity of this claim. That is, in fact, the essence of the dispute in the theory of knowledge between the elements of demonstrative reason and sensory testing). It is an injustice for the philosopher to believe that he alone is addressed by the intellectual discourse in the Qur'an without others, because the Qur'an's discourse is for everyone, as it relies on general common sense and common sense (fitra), whereas philosophy is highly suspicious of general common sense and often attempts to refute this idea (2). The intellect, when performing its roles, performs them in two main functions: First: It organizes sensory perception and transforms it into knowledge, called by rationalists "posteriori knowledge," i.e., after experience and experimentation. Second: When the intellect discovers knowledge independently of the senses, this knowledge is called "a priori knowledge," i.e., before experience, meaning it is based on the concept of primary ideas, or theory, or the a priori, where it is considered to emanate from the intellect itself as a source of knowledge (3).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature and functions of the intellect within an Islamic epistemological framework. The author begins by citing a Qur'anic verse (39:18) that praises those who listen to speech and follow the best of it, identifying them as people of understanding (al-bab). This serves as a foundation for arguing that Islamic education should prioritize intellectual excellence. The author then poses key questions: In which domain does the intellect operate—the unseen (ghayb) or the visible (shahada)? How does it relate to revelation? The author first clarifies that the intellect is a faculty that operates behind the senses and is the real agent in knowledge acquisition. Its role extends to the unseen, either through the visible world or by inferring faith in God. An objection is anticipated: some argue that the unseen is only known through revelation, not intellect. The author responds by noting that there are signs (āyāt) in the horizons and souls that testify to faith, and these are accessible to the intellect. However, the intellect's reach is limited to broad outlines; detailed knowledge of the unseen requires revelation. The author then traces the root of the problem to the classic dispute between empiricists and rationalists. While a common person accepts sensory data as self-evident, the philosopher demands rational proof. This leads to an erroneous belief that only philosophers are addressed by the Qur'an's intellectual discourse. The author rejects this, emphasizing that the Qur'an addresses everyone, relying on common sense (fitra). Philosophy's skepticism toward common sense is misguided. Finally, the author delineates two primary functions of the intellect: (1) organizing sensory perception into posteriori knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-ba'diyya), i.e., knowledge after experience; and (2) discovering knowledge independently of the senses, called a priori knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-qabliyya), which originates from the intellect itself as a source. This classification underscores the intellect's dual role in processing empirical data and in grasping innate or rational truths.

أولو الألباب

D) It should be limited to memorization of the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's praise of understanding is a foundational mandate for intellectual education in Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another Qur'anic verse that emphasizes the use of intellect and explain how it could be used to justify a specific educational practice.
?
Write a short paragraph refuting the claim that Islam discourages independent thinking, using evidence from the passage.

The Intellect's Domain: Visible and Unseen
Author defines intellect's epistemic reach into unseen realm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intellect's role in inferring knowledge of the unseen through the visible and identify its limits.

Core Idea
The intellect can infer the unseen from visible signs, but the details of the unseen require revelation.

The author addresses a key tension: can the intellect know anything about the unseen? He argues that the intellect's role extends beyond the visible world to infer faith through signs, yet detailed knowledge remains the province of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two realms
The passage mentions 'alam al-shahada (visible world) and 'alam al-ghayb (unseen world). Understand that the intellect operates in both.
2
Understand the objection
The author anticipates an objection: some claim that only revelation can know the unseen. Find this objection in the Arabic.
Look for 'رب قائل يعترض'.
3
Find the author's response
The author responds that there are evidences (signs in horizons and souls) from the visible world that allow the intellect to infer truths about the unseen.
4
Note the limitation
The intellect only grasps broad outlines; detailed knowledge of the unseen is from revelation.
The passage says 'العقل لا يتعدى حدوده' regarding details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
الَّذِينَ يَسْتَمِعُونَ الْقَوْلَ فَيَتَّبِعُونَ أَحْسَنَهُ أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ هَدَاهُمُ اللهُ وَأُولَئِكَ هُمْ أُولُو الأَلْباب ﴾ [الزمر: ١٨/٣٩]. وهذا يدلّ بالتأكيد على أن من المفترض أن تتوجّه السياسات التعليمية المنبثقة عن التربية الإسلامية إلى تكريم ذوي التفوق العقلي من المتعلمين، وأن تولي المناهج التعليمية اهتماماً أكبر بجوانب التربية العقلية، كما ينبغي أن تتاح فرص لتقديم الأنشطة ذات الطابع العقلي من ثقافية وفكرية وسواهما، وأن تُقدّم الحوافز الدافعة لدعم ذلك التفاعل، وتلك الأنشطة.
طبيعة عمل العقل
إذا كان العقل على ذلك النحو من الأهمية فما طبيعة عمله؟ بمعنى في أي عالم يقوم العقل بوظائفه : أفي عالم الغيب أم الشهادة؟ وكيف يتجسد دوره في الوحي؟ وكيف يتجسد دور الوحي فيه؟ وحتى يمكننا تحديد الإجابة عن هذين السؤالين يحسن بنا أن ندرك ابتداءً أن العقل ملكة واسعة تجعله يقف وراء الحواس، باعتباره العنصر الحقيقي في عملية الإدراك أو المعرفة العقلية والعلمية، ولذلك فإن دوره المعرفي يتعدى عالم الشهادة إلى عالم الغيب، سواء كان ذلك عن طريق عالم الشهادة نفسه، أم كان عن طريق عالم الغيب في الاستدلال على الإيمان بالله تعالى (۱) - على سبيل المثال - ولكن رُبَّ قائل يعترض على هذا في ضوء ما تم تقريره عند الحديث عن ميدان المعرفة في عالم الغيب، وأن طريقها ليس العقل، كما أنه ليس الحواس، بل الوحي سبيل معرفتها الوحيد. وهنا نرى ضرورة التذكير بما سبقت الإشارة إليه عند الحديث عن ميدان المعرفة في عالم الغيب أيضاً من أن ثمة دلائل تشهد لهذا الإيمان سواء تلك التي تأتي نتائج تلقائية للإيمان بالله تعالى أو تلك التي تبرز في ميداني الآفاق والأنفس من عالم الشهادة، وهي تمثل جزءاً من المشهد الطويل الذي تتم بعض فصوله في عالم الشهادة، وتبقى فصوله الأخرى مطوية في إطار عالم الغيب لأن معرفتنا بها لا تتعدى الخطوط العريضة لعالم الغيب، أما بقية التفصيلات فإن العقل لا يتعدى حدوده إزاءها، إذ إن مصدر ذلك وسبيل معرفته الوحي غير. لا ولكن المشكلة تعود - في جذورها - هنا إلى أصل ذلك النزاع الذي دارت رحاه بين الحسيين والعقليين. فالمشكلة - لدى الفيلسوف مثلاً - هي أن الرجل العادي يؤمن بالمحسوسات على أنها حقائق في ذاتها، بينما يريد الفيلسوف التأكد بالبراهين العقلية من صحة هذا الزعم؟ ذلك - في الواقع جوهر النزاع في نظرية المعرفة بين عنصري العقل البرهاني والاختبار الحسي). ومن قبيل الاعتساف أن يعتقد الفيلسوف أنه وحده المعني بالخطاب العقلي في القرآن دون سواه، ذلك لأن خطاب القرآن للكافة، لأنه يعتمد على البداهة العامة، والحس المشترك (الفطرة)، في حين ترتاب الفلسفة في البداهة العامة ارتياباً كبيراً، وتحاول دحض هذه الفكرة في كثير من الأحيان(٢). إن العقل إذ يؤدي أدواره فإنه يؤديها في وظيفتين رئيستين هما: الأولى: ينظم الإدراك الحسي فيحوله إلى معرفة وتسمى عند العقليين معرفـة بَعْدِيَّة) أي بعد الخبرة والتجربة. الثانية: حين يكتشف العقل المعرفة في استقلال عن الحواس، فتسمى هذه المعرفة (قبليَّة) أي قبل التجربة، أي إنها تقوم على مفهوم الأفكار الأولية، أو النظرية، أو القبلية، حيث تعد نابعة من العقل ذاته باعتباره مصدراً للمعرفة(٣).
Chapter Three: Section Two - The Intellect "Those who listen to the word and follow the best of it; those are the ones whom Allah has guided, and those are the people of understanding" [Az-Zumar 39:18]. This certainly indicates that educational policies emanating from Islamic education should honor those with intellectual superiority among learners, and that curricula should pay greater attention to aspects of intellectual education, and opportunities should be provided for presenting intellectual activities of a cultural and intellectual nature and others, and incentives should be offered to support that interaction and those activities. The Nature of the Intellect's Work: If the intellect is of such importance, what is the nature of its work? That is, in which world does the intellect perform its functions: in the world of the unseen or the visible? And how is its role embodied in revelation? And how is the role of revelation embodied in it? So that we can determine the answer to these two questions, it is good for us to realize initially that the intellect is a vast faculty that makes it stand behind the senses, as it is the real element in the process of perception or intellectual and scientific knowledge. Therefore, its cognitive role extends beyond the visible world to the unseen world, whether that is through the visible world itself, or through the unseen world in inferring faith in Allah the Exalted (1) - for example. But perhaps someone may object to this in light of what has been settled in the discussion about the field of knowledge in the unseen world, and that its path is not the intellect, nor the senses, but revelation is its only way of knowing. Here we see the necessity of recalling what was previously referred to in the discussion about the field of knowledge in the unseen world as well: that there are evidences that testify to this faith, whether those that come as spontaneous results of faith in Allah the Exalted or those that appear in the fields of horizons and souls from the visible world. They represent part of the long scene, some of whose chapters take place in the visible world, while its other chapters remain folded within the framework of the unseen world because our knowledge of them does not go beyond the broad outlines of the unseen world. As for the rest of the details, the intellect does not go beyond its limits regarding them, for the source of that and the path to knowing it is revelation, not the intellect. But the problem - in its roots - here goes back to the origin of that dispute that raged between empiricists and rationalists. The problem - for the philosopher, for example - is that the common person believes in sensory objects as truths in themselves, while the philosopher wants to verify with rational proofs the validity of this claim. That is, in fact, the essence of the dispute in the theory of knowledge between the elements of demonstrative reason and sensory testing). It is an injustice for the philosopher to believe that he alone is addressed by the intellectual discourse in the Qur'an without others, because the Qur'an's discourse is for everyone, as it relies on general common sense and common sense (fitra), whereas philosophy is highly suspicious of general common sense and often attempts to refute this idea (2). The intellect, when performing its roles, performs them in two main functions: First: It organizes sensory perception and transforms it into knowledge, called by rationalists "posteriori knowledge," i.e., after experience and experimentation. Second: When the intellect discovers knowledge independently of the senses, this knowledge is called "a priori knowledge," i.e., before experience, meaning it is based on the concept of primary ideas, or theory, or the a priori, where it is considered to emanate from the intellect itself as a source of knowledge (3).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature and functions of the intellect within an Islamic epistemological framework. The author begins by citing a Qur'anic verse (39:18) that praises those who listen to speech and follow the best of it, identifying them as people of understanding (al-bab). This serves as a foundation for arguing that Islamic education should prioritize intellectual excellence. The author then poses key questions: In which domain does the intellect operate—the unseen (ghayb) or the visible (shahada)? How does it relate to revelation? The author first clarifies that the intellect is a faculty that operates behind the senses and is the real agent in knowledge acquisition. Its role extends to the unseen, either through the visible world or by inferring faith in God. An objection is anticipated: some argue that the unseen is only known through revelation, not intellect. The author responds by noting that there are signs (āyāt) in the horizons and souls that testify to faith, and these are accessible to the intellect. However, the intellect's reach is limited to broad outlines; detailed knowledge of the unseen requires revelation. The author then traces the root of the problem to the classic dispute between empiricists and rationalists. While a common person accepts sensory data as self-evident, the philosopher demands rational proof. This leads to an erroneous belief that only philosophers are addressed by the Qur'an's intellectual discourse. The author rejects this, emphasizing that the Qur'an addresses everyone, relying on common sense (fitra). Philosophy's skepticism toward common sense is misguided. Finally, the author delineates two primary functions of the intellect: (1) organizing sensory perception into posteriori knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-ba'diyya), i.e., knowledge after experience; and (2) discovering knowledge independently of the senses, called a priori knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-qabliyya), which originates from the intellect itself as a source. This classification underscores the intellect's dual role in processing empirical data and in grasping innate or rational truths.

Key Terms
عالم الغيب
عالم الشهادة
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the limit of the intellect's knowledge regarding the unseen world?
A) The intellect can know everything about the unseen.
B) The intellect can only know the unseen through revelation.
C) The intellect can grasp broad outlines but not details of the unseen.
D) The intellect has no access to the unseen at all.

Key Takeaway
The intellect infers broad truths about the unseen from visible signs, but revelation provides the detailed knowledge.

Dispute Between Empiricists and Rationalists: The Qur'an's Reliance on Fitrah
Author critiques philosophical skepticism and upholds fitrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the difference between the Qur'anic epistemology, which relies on fitrah, and philosophical rationalism, which demands proof for everything.

Core Idea
The Qur'an speaks to all humans using common sense, not philosophical superiority.

The author contrasts the Qur'anic approach with that of some rationalist philosophers. While philosophers doubt the reliability of common sense and sensory knowledge, the Qur'an takes fitrah as a starting point. This lesson explores that contrast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting positions
Find where the passage contrasts the common person ('āmmī) and the philosopher. The common person trusts sensory truths; the philosopher demands rational proof.
2
Understand the author's view
The author sides with the Qur'anic approach: it relies on general common sense (al-badāha al-‘āmma) and fitrah.
The word 'fitrah' appears in parentheses.
3
Recognize the criticism
The author calls it an 'injustice' (i‘tisāf) for the philosopher to assume only philosophers are addressed by the Qur'an's rational discourse.
4
Reflect on implications
This shows that Islamic epistemology is inclusive, based on innate human disposition, not elitist skepticism.

The Two Functions of the Intellect: A Priori and A Posteriori Knowledge
Author delineates the two epistemic functions of the intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a priori and a posteriori knowledge in an Islamic context and identify where each is used.

Core Idea
The intellect has two main functions: organizing sensory experience and discovering truths independently of experience.

The passage concludes by outlining two distinct functions of the intellect. This lesson unpacks that classification, which is essential for understanding how knowledge is acquired both from the world and from innate rational principles.

Guided Path
1
Find the two functions in the Arabic
Locate the sentences beginning with 'الأولى: ينظم الإدراك الحسي...' and 'الثانية: حين يكتشف العقل المعرفة...'.
2
Understand the terminology
The first function yields 'ba'diyya' (posteriori) knowledge, the second 'qabliyya' (a priori). Note that the author uses these terms from modern philosophy.
Classical terms: badihiyyat or awwaliyyat for a priori.
3
Identify examples from Islamic sciences
A posteriori: empirical observations leading to proofs of God's wisdom. A priori: self-evident logical principles like non-contradiction used in usul al-fiqh.
4
Explain why both are needed
Islamic epistemology integrates both: sensory data requires rational organization, and innate principles provide foundational axioms.

الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
الَّذِينَ يَسْتَمِعُونَ الْقَوْلَ فَيَتَّبِعُونَ أَحْسَنَهُ أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ هَدَاهُمُ اللهُ وَأُولَئِكَ هُمْ أُولُو الأَلْباب ﴾ [الزمر: ١٨/٣٩]. وهذا يدلّ بالتأكيد على أن من المفترض أن تتوجّه السياسات التعليمية المنبثقة عن التربية الإسلامية إلى تكريم ذوي التفوق العقلي من المتعلمين، وأن تولي المناهج التعليمية اهتماماً أكبر بجوانب التربية العقلية، كما ينبغي أن تتاح فرص لتقديم الأنشطة ذات الطابع العقلي من ثقافية وفكرية وسواهما، وأن تُقدّم الحوافز الدافعة لدعم ذلك التفاعل، وتلك الأنشطة.
طبيعة عمل العقل
إذا كان العقل على ذلك النحو من الأهمية فما طبيعة عمله؟ بمعنى في أي عالم يقوم العقل بوظائفه : أفي عالم الغيب أم الشهادة؟ وكيف يتجسد دوره في الوحي؟ وكيف يتجسد دور الوحي فيه؟ وحتى يمكننا تحديد الإجابة عن هذين السؤالين يحسن بنا أن ندرك ابتداءً أن العقل ملكة واسعة تجعله يقف وراء الحواس، باعتباره العنصر الحقيقي في عملية الإدراك أو المعرفة العقلية والعلمية، ولذلك فإن دوره المعرفي يتعدى عالم الشهادة إلى عالم الغيب، سواء كان ذلك عن طريق عالم الشهادة نفسه، أم كان عن طريق عالم الغيب في الاستدلال على الإيمان بالله تعالى (۱) - على سبيل المثال - ولكن رُبَّ قائل يعترض على هذا في ضوء ما تم تقريره عند الحديث عن ميدان المعرفة في عالم الغيب، وأن طريقها ليس العقل، كما أنه ليس الحواس، بل الوحي سبيل معرفتها الوحيد. وهنا نرى ضرورة التذكير بما سبقت الإشارة إليه عند الحديث عن ميدان المعرفة في عالم الغيب أيضاً من أن ثمة دلائل تشهد لهذا الإيمان سواء تلك التي تأتي نتائج تلقائية للإيمان بالله تعالى أو تلك التي تبرز في ميداني الآفاق والأنفس من عالم الشهادة، وهي تمثل جزءاً من المشهد الطويل الذي تتم بعض فصوله في عالم الشهادة، وتبقى فصوله الأخرى مطوية في إطار عالم الغيب لأن معرفتنا بها لا تتعدى الخطوط العريضة لعالم الغيب، أما بقية التفصيلات فإن العقل لا يتعدى حدوده إزاءها، إذ إن مصدر ذلك وسبيل معرفته الوحي غير. لا ولكن المشكلة تعود - في جذورها - هنا إلى أصل ذلك النزاع الذي دارت رحاه بين الحسيين والعقليين. فالمشكلة - لدى الفيلسوف مثلاً - هي أن الرجل العادي يؤمن بالمحسوسات على أنها حقائق في ذاتها، بينما يريد الفيلسوف التأكد بالبراهين العقلية من صحة هذا الزعم؟ ذلك - في الواقع جوهر النزاع في نظرية المعرفة بين عنصري العقل البرهاني والاختبار الحسي). ومن قبيل الاعتساف أن يعتقد الفيلسوف أنه وحده المعني بالخطاب العقلي في القرآن دون سواه، ذلك لأن خطاب القرآن للكافة، لأنه يعتمد على البداهة العامة، والحس المشترك (الفطرة)، في حين ترتاب الفلسفة في البداهة العامة ارتياباً كبيراً، وتحاول دحض هذه الفكرة في كثير من الأحيان(٢). إن العقل إذ يؤدي أدواره فإنه يؤديها في وظيفتين رئيستين هما: الأولى: ينظم الإدراك الحسي فيحوله إلى معرفة وتسمى عند العقليين معرفـة بَعْدِيَّة) أي بعد الخبرة والتجربة. الثانية: حين يكتشف العقل المعرفة في استقلال عن الحواس، فتسمى هذه المعرفة (قبليَّة) أي قبل التجربة، أي إنها تقوم على مفهوم الأفكار الأولية، أو النظرية، أو القبلية، حيث تعد نابعة من العقل ذاته باعتباره مصدراً للمعرفة(٣).
Chapter Three: Section Two - The Intellect "Those who listen to the word and follow the best of it; those are the ones whom Allah has guided, and those are the people of understanding" [Az-Zumar 39:18]. This certainly indicates that educational policies emanating from Islamic education should honor those with intellectual superiority among learners, and that curricula should pay greater attention to aspects of intellectual education, and opportunities should be provided for presenting intellectual activities of a cultural and intellectual nature and others, and incentives should be offered to support that interaction and those activities. The Nature of the Intellect's Work: If the intellect is of such importance, what is the nature of its work? That is, in which world does the intellect perform its functions: in the world of the unseen or the visible? And how is its role embodied in revelation? And how is the role of revelation embodied in it? So that we can determine the answer to these two questions, it is good for us to realize initially that the intellect is a vast faculty that makes it stand behind the senses, as it is the real element in the process of perception or intellectual and scientific knowledge. Therefore, its cognitive role extends beyond the visible world to the unseen world, whether that is through the visible world itself, or through the unseen world in inferring faith in Allah the Exalted (1) - for example. But perhaps someone may object to this in light of what has been settled in the discussion about the field of knowledge in the unseen world, and that its path is not the intellect, nor the senses, but revelation is its only way of knowing. Here we see the necessity of recalling what was previously referred to in the discussion about the field of knowledge in the unseen world as well: that there are evidences that testify to this faith, whether those that come as spontaneous results of faith in Allah the Exalted or those that appear in the fields of horizons and souls from the visible world. They represent part of the long scene, some of whose chapters take place in the visible world, while its other chapters remain folded within the framework of the unseen world because our knowledge of them does not go beyond the broad outlines of the unseen world. As for the rest of the details, the intellect does not go beyond its limits regarding them, for the source of that and the path to knowing it is revelation, not the intellect. But the problem - in its roots - here goes back to the origin of that dispute that raged between empiricists and rationalists. The problem - for the philosopher, for example - is that the common person believes in sensory objects as truths in themselves, while the philosopher wants to verify with rational proofs the validity of this claim. That is, in fact, the essence of the dispute in the theory of knowledge between the elements of demonstrative reason and sensory testing). It is an injustice for the philosopher to believe that he alone is addressed by the intellectual discourse in the Qur'an without others, because the Qur'an's discourse is for everyone, as it relies on general common sense and common sense (fitra), whereas philosophy is highly suspicious of general common sense and often attempts to refute this idea (2). The intellect, when performing its roles, performs them in two main functions: First: It organizes sensory perception and transforms it into knowledge, called by rationalists "posteriori knowledge," i.e., after experience and experimentation. Second: When the intellect discovers knowledge independently of the senses, this knowledge is called "a priori knowledge," i.e., before experience, meaning it is based on the concept of primary ideas, or theory, or the a priori, where it is considered to emanate from the intellect itself as a source of knowledge (3).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature and functions of the intellect within an Islamic epistemological framework. The author begins by citing a Qur'anic verse (39:18) that praises those who listen to speech and follow the best of it, identifying them as people of understanding (al-bab). This serves as a foundation for arguing that Islamic education should prioritize intellectual excellence. The author then poses key questions: In which domain does the intellect operate—the unseen (ghayb) or the visible (shahada)? How does it relate to revelation? The author first clarifies that the intellect is a faculty that operates behind the senses and is the real agent in knowledge acquisition. Its role extends to the unseen, either through the visible world or by inferring faith in God. An objection is anticipated: some argue that the unseen is only known through revelation, not intellect. The author responds by noting that there are signs (āyāt) in the horizons and souls that testify to faith, and these are accessible to the intellect. However, the intellect's reach is limited to broad outlines; detailed knowledge of the unseen requires revelation. The author then traces the root of the problem to the classic dispute between empiricists and rationalists. While a common person accepts sensory data as self-evident, the philosopher demands rational proof. This leads to an erroneous belief that only philosophers are addressed by the Qur'an's intellectual discourse. The author rejects this, emphasizing that the Qur'an addresses everyone, relying on common sense (fitra). Philosophy's skepticism toward common sense is misguided. Finally, the author delineates two primary functions of the intellect: (1) organizing sensory perception into posteriori knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-ba'diyya), i.e., knowledge after experience; and (2) discovering knowledge independently of the senses, called a priori knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-qabliyya), which originates from the intellect itself as a source. This classification underscores the intellect's dual role in processing empirical data and in grasping innate or rational truths.

Key Terms
المعرفة البعدية
المعرفة القبلية

A) Knowledge that comes from sensory experience and experimentation.

Key Takeaway
The intellect processes sensory data (a posteriori) and grasps rational truths (a priori); both are integral to Islamic epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Functions of the Intellect and the Role of Attention
Author defines intellect's functions and introduces attention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the active nature of the intellect and its higher processes, and explain why attention is essential for knowledge acquisition as per the Quran.

Core Idea
The intellect is an active faculty, not a passive possession; attention is the gateway to learning.

This lesson explores the Islamic epistemological framework of the intellect ('aql) as presented in the source passage. The author emphasises that the intellect is not a passive receptacle but an active faculty responsible for higher cognitive processes such as thinking, understanding, and deduction. Furthermore, he introduces attention (tanabbuh) as a necessary condition for effective learning, grounding it in Quranic commands to listen attentively. Understanding these functions is crucial for any student of Islamic sciences, as it lays the foundation for how knowledge is acquired and validated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the active nature of the intellect
Notice that the author states the Quran uses 'aql only to indicate an action or deed. Find the phrase 'للدلالة على عمل وفعل' and understand that the intellect must be used to be effective; it is not merely a latent capacity.
Compare with the common misconception that intellect is a passive repository.
2
List the higher processes of the intellect
From the opening sentences, extract the list of higher processes: thinking (tafkīr), remembering (tadhakkur), understanding (tafahhum), learning (ta'allum), imagining (takhayyul), and perceiving relationships (idrāk al-‘alāqāt). Note that these are considered goals of knowledge, not knowledge itself.
3
Distinguish between having intellect and using it
Find the contrast: some people do not use these functions due to neglect (ta‘ṭīl), not because they lack them. Refer to the Quranic verse 67:10 where disbelievers say 'Had we but listened or used our intellect...'—they had intellect but did not use it.
This distinction is key to understanding human responsibility in knowledge acquisition.
4
Recognise attention as a prerequisite for learning
Read the section on attention (al-tanabbuh). The author states that without attention, information cannot be perceived, learned, or retrieved. Note the example of a lecture where inattention prevents comprehension.
Attention is not just a psychological concept but is rooted in the Quran.
5
Connect the concept to Quranic commands
Locate the two Quranic verses cited: 7:204 (listen to the Quran and be silent) and 50:37 (a reminder for whoever has a heart or gives ear while present). These verses command attentive listening, showing that attention is divinely mandated for learning.
This demonstrates that the intellect's function of reception (talqī) requires active attention.

إن ذلك يقودنا إلى تحديد أبرز وظائف العقل عبر عملياته العليا كالتفكير والتذكر والتفهم والتعلم والتخيّل، وإدراك العلاقات بين الأشياء المحسوسة من جهة وبين المجردات من جهة أخرى. ومع أن بعض العلماء قديماً عد هذه المعاني مرادفة لحقيقة العلم أو قريباً من ذلك، لكن التدقيق في ذلك يؤكد أنها إنما تمثل أهدافاً معرفية تربوية عامة للعلم، لا أنها العلم بعينه. ومن الملاحظ أن هذه الوظائف لا تستخدم من قبل بعض الناس بسبب تعطيلهم لها، لا أنهم يفقدونها أو ناقصو الأهلية الخلقية مثلاً. فإن القرآن الكريم لم يستخدم لفظ العقل في القرآن الكريم إلا للدلالة على عمل وفعل - كما سبقت الإشارة، ولا نجده يصف الكافرين بعدم وجود العقل عندهم - بل نجده يقول فيهم ولا يعقلون وقد جاء على لسانهم أيضاً: وَقَالُوا لَوْ كُنَّا نَسْمَعُ أَوْ نَعْقِلُ... [الملك: ١٠/٦٧]، وذلك يشير إلى وظيفة العقل. وحسب استقراء بعض الباحثين فإن النظر بمعنى العلم ورد في نحو (٢٣) آية والإبصار بمعنى العلم ورد في نحو من (٢٣) آية كذلك. والفقه بمعنى الفهم في (٢٣) آية أيضاً. وهكذا فإن الدور الرئيس للعقل كجهاز حاكم للمعرفة البشرية يتمثل في التلقي والفهم والاستنباط. وذلك يشير إلى تلبية تلك الأهداف المعرفية المشار إليها غير مرة فيما سبق. وثمة عمليات عقلية عليا كالتنبه والتذكر والتفكر يجمل أن نشير إليها بقدر من التفصيل لما لها من أدوار مميزة في عملية المعرفة والتعلم بوجه خاص، وذلك على النحو التالي: أولاً: التنبه. من المقرر عند علماء النفس أن للانتباه دوراً هاماً في عملية التعلم واكتساب المعرفة وتحصيل العلم. فعدم الانتباه إلى محاضرة ما يجعل المستمع غير قادر على إدراك ما تضمنته من المعلومات، ومن ثم فلن يتمكن من تعلمها أو استرجاعها فيما بعد. ولذلك كان عامل الانتباه لدى المعلمين في إثارة انتباه تلاميذهم على غاية الأهمية في عملية استيعاب الدرس وتحصيله. وهذا ما أشار إليه القرآن بقوله: وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنْصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ [الأعراف: ٢٠٤/٧]. وإنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَى لِمَنْ كانَ لَهُ قَلْبٌ أَوْ أَلْقَى السَّمْعَ وَهُوَ شَهِيدٌ [ق: ٣٧/٥٠].
That leads us to identify the most prominent functions of the intellect through its higher processes such as thinking, remembering, understanding, learning, imagining, and perceiving relationships between sensory objects on one hand and abstractions on the other. Although some earlier scholars counted these meanings as synonymous with the reality of knowledge or close to that, careful examination confirms that they represent general educational and cognitive goals of knowledge, not knowledge itself. It is noteworthy that these functions are not used by some people due to their disabling them, not that they lack them or are deficient in natural ability. For the Noble Qur'an did not use the word 'aql (intellect) except to indicate an action and deed—as previously indicated—and we do not find it describing the disbelievers as lacking intellect; rather, it says about them: 'they do not understand' and it also came on their tongues: 'Had we but listened or used our intellect...' [Surah al-Mulk 67:10], and that indicates the function of the intellect. According to the induction of some researchers, 'nazar' (consideration) in the sense of knowledge appears in about 23 verses, and 'ibsar' (seeing) in the sense of knowledge appears in about 23 verses as well. And 'fiqh' (understanding) in the sense of comprehension appears in 23 verses too. Thus the main role of the intellect as a governing device for human knowledge is reception, understanding, and deduction. That indicates fulfilling those cognitive goals mentioned several times before. There are also higher mental processes such as attention, remembering, and reflection that we should mention in some detail due to their distinctive roles in the process of knowledge and learning in particular, as follows: First: Attention. It is established among psychologists that attention has an important role in the process of learning, knowledge acquisition, and attainment of knowledge. Lack of attention to a lecture makes the listener unable to perceive the information it contains, and subsequently he will not be able to learn it or retrieve it later. Therefore, the factor of attention among teachers in arousing the attention of their students is of utmost importance in the process of comprehending and acquiring the lesson. This is what the Qur'an pointed out by His saying: 'And when the Qur'an is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy' [Surah al-A'raf 7:204]. 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart or gives ear while he is a witness' [Surah Qaf 50:37].
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the functions of the intellect ('aql) in an Islamic epistemological framework. The author begins by listing higher cognitive processes: thinking, remembering, understanding, learning, imagining, and perceiving relationships between sensory and abstract objects. He notes that earlier scholars equated these with knowledge itself, but clarifies that they are actually general educational objectives, not knowledge per se. The author then emphasizes that these functions are present in all humans but are not utilized by some due to neglect, not because they lack the faculty—citing Qur'anic verses that reproach disbelievers for not using their intellect (e.g., 67:10). Statistical inductions from the Qur'an are given: words like 'nazar' (consideration), 'ibsar' (seeing), and 'fiqh' (understanding) each appear about 23 times in the sense of knowledge. The main role of the intellect is summarized as reception (talqī), understanding (fahm), and deduction (istinbāṭ). Finally, the author introduces attention (tanabbuh) as a crucial higher mental process for learning, supported by Qur'anic injunctions to listen attentively (7:204) and the reminder for those who have a heart and listen (50:37). This discussion grounds logical reasoning in the prior act of attentive reception, essential for valid inference.

The Interplay of Senses and Intellect in Knowledge Acquisition
Author discusses inductive and historical methods in the Quran, emphasizing cooperation of senses and intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the senses and intellect cooperate in knowledge acquisition according to the Quranic worldview, and identify the inductive and historical methods used in Quranic reasoning.

Core Idea
Senses and intellect are inseparable; senses provide raw data, intellect processes it into knowledge.

This passage explores how the Quran consistently links sensory perception with intellectual reasoning. Through examples from stories of Prophet Ibrahim, ‘Ad, and Thamud, the author demonstrates that knowledge arises from the interplay of empirical observation and rational inference. Understanding this epistemological framework is crucial for appreciating how Islamic sciences build knowledge from both sense and intellect.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the inductive method
Identify how the passage uses particular instances (e.g., giving life, subjugating the universe) to infer a universal truth (Allah's absolute power). This is inductive reasoning.
The verse from Al-Baqarah 2:258 provides the context of a debate where Ibrahim uses this method.
2
Identify the historical method
Note how the passage cites ruins and past destruction (‘Ad and Thamud) as observable evidence that leads to intellectual conclusions about divine punishment. This combines sensory observation (seeing remnants) with historical reasoning.
Verses from Al-Ankabut and As-Sajdah are used.
3
Understand the necessary connection (تلازم)
Observe that the author states that hearing and sight are gateways to the intellect, and that both are required for knowledge. The verses from Yunus show that without intellect, sensory input is useless.
The term 'telazum' implies mutual dependence.
4
Trace the role of intuitive sciences
Read the claim that acquired knowledge (علوم كسبية) depends on intuitive knowledge (علوم بديهية), which in turn depends on concepts formed with sensory help. This establishes the senses as the original cause of all knowledge.
Intuitive sciences are self-evident foundations like 'the whole is greater than the part'.
5
Evaluate the status of animals
Note the author's remark that animals have senses but lack intellect (heart), so they do not acquire knowledge in the human sense. This clarifies the unique human responsibility.
The passage ends with a critique of a claim about animal intellect, though incomplete.

يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِها مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ فَبُهِتَ الَّذِي كَفَرَ وَاللهُ لا يَهْدِي الْقَوْمَ الظَّالِمِينَ (البقرة: 258/2). وهنا يبرز المنهج الاستقرائي في الاستدلال على كمال قدرة الله تعالى وإطلاقيتها، فإن مظاهر الإحياء وتسخير الكون، دلائل جزئية، تقود إلى الإيمان بحقيقة كلية هي المحيي والمسخر لذلك وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى- وذلك من فلسفة التربية الإسلامية وهو الإله - جل وعلا - ويلاحظ أنه إلى جانب الحس المستنتج من منهج الاستقراء يأتي العقل الذي فرض أسلوب التنزل مع الخصم مرحلياً، بهدف إيقاعه في مصيدة الاستسلام، وذلك يتطلب تفكيراً ملياً، وتدبيراً فاحصاً، وعمق نظر ، وذلك جميعه من لوازم عمليات العقل العليا، وهو أرقى مستوى من مستويات الأهداف حسب تصنيف (بلوم) لها. وقال تعالى: ﴿وَعاداً وَثَمُودَ وَقَدْ تَبَيَّنَ لَكُمْ مِنْ مَساكِنِهِمْ وَزَيَّنَ لَهُمُ الشَّيْطَانُ أَعْمَالَهُمْ فَصَدَّهُمْ عَن السَّبِيلِ وَكَانُوا مُستبصرين﴾ [العنكبوت: 38/29] أَوَلَمْ يَهْدِ لَهُمْ كَمْ أَهْلَكْنَا مِنْ قَبْلِهِمْ مِنَ الْقُرُونِ يَمْشُونَ فِي مَسَاكِنِهِمْ إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَآياتٍ أَفَلا يَسْمَعُونَ [السجدة: 26/32]. وهنا يلاحظ استخدام المنهج التاريخي القائم على تتبع أحداث الماضي بعد مشاهدة أطلالها، من خلال إلحاق المشاهدة بإعمال العقل. وندرك صورة التلازم القائم بين الحس والعقل حتى لكأن السمع والبصر المستخدمين بدقة هما المنفذان المعتبران للعقل ﴿وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَسْتَمِعُونَ إِلَيْكَ أَفَأَنْتَ تُسْمِعُ الصُّمَّ وَلَوْ كَانُوا لا يَعْقِلُونَ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَنظُرُ إِلَيْكَ أَفَأَنْتَ تَهْدِي الْعُمْيَ وَلَوْ كَانُوا لا يُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [يونس: 42/10 - 43]. وقد خلق الله الإنسان مزوّداً بالحواس والعقل معاً، ولذلك فإن مسؤوليته مناطة بهما معاً: ﴿وَاللهُ أَخْرَجَكُمْ مِنْ بُطُون أُمَّهَاتِكُمْ لا تَعْلَمُونَ شَيْئاً وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ السَّمْعَ وَالأَبْصَارَ وَالأَفْئِدَةَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَشْكُرُونَ﴾ [النحل: 78/16] وَهُوَ الَّذِي أَنْشَأَ لَكُمُ السَّمْعَ وَالأَبْصارَ وَالْأَفْئِدَةَ قَلِيلاً ما تَشْكُرُونَ [المؤمنون: 78/23] ثُمَّ سَوَاهُ وَنَفَخَ فِيهِ مِنْ رُوحِهِ وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ السَّمْعَ وَالأَبْصَارَ وَالأَفْئِدَةَ قَلِيلاً ما تَشْكُرُونَ [السجدة: 9/32] وَلا تَقْفُ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ إِنَّ السَّمْعَ وَالْبَصَرَ وَالْفُؤادَ كُلُّ أُولَئِكَ كان عَنْهُ مَسْؤُولاً [الإسراء: 36/17]. ومن الجدير ملاحظته في هذه الآيات أن ذكر الأفئدة يشير إلى معنى العقل، لأنه مقر الإدراك كله فهو الذي تنقل إليه الحواس مدركاتها، وهي العلم بالتصورات المفردة. والإشارة بينة إلى أنه لا يمكن اكتساب العلوم الكسبية إلا بواسطة العلوم البديهية النظري والضروري. وحصول العلوم البديهية يتحقق عند تصور موضوعاتها ومجالاتها. وحدوث هذه التصورات إنما يكون بسبب إعانة الحواس على جزئياتها. وبذلك تصير الحواس الخمس السبب الأصلي لحدوث هذه العلوم. ويكون السمع والبصر أول الحواس تحصيلاً للتصورات وأهمها. صحيح أن الآيات ميّزت الإنسان عن الحيوان بالعقل إذ لا فؤاد عند الأخير رغم امتلاكه للحواس، ولكن لربما كان من غير الدقيـق مـا ذهـب إليـه أحـد
He brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west, so the disbeliever was confounded, and Allah does not guide the wrongdoing people (Al-Baqarah 2:258). Here emerges the inductive method in deducing the perfection and absoluteness of Allah's power; for the manifestations of giving life and subjugating the universe are particular indications leading to belief in a universal truth: the One who gives life and subjugates it is Allah, glorified and exalted. That is from the philosophy of Islamic education: He is God—mighty and sublime. It is noted that alongside the sense derived from the inductive method comes the intellect, which imposed the method of gradual descent with the opponent, aimed at entrapping him in surrender. This requires deep thought, careful planning, and profound insight, all of which are necessary for higher intellectual operations, and it is the highest level of objectives according to Bloom's taxonomy. And He said, 'And 'Ad and Thamud, and it has become clear to you from their dwellings, and Satan beautified their deeds for them and turned them away from the path, though they were insightful' (Al-Ankabut 29:38). 'Have they not been guided by how many generations We destroyed before them, among whose dwellings they walk? Indeed in that are signs; do they not hear?' (As-Sajdah 32:26). Here the use of the historical method based on tracing past events after observing their ruins is noted, by annexing observation to the exercise of the intellect. We perceive the image of the necessary connection between sense and intellect, such that hearing and sight, when used accurately, are the considered gateways to the intellect. 'And among them are those who listen to you; can you make the deaf hear even if they do not reason? And among them are those who look at you; can you guide the blind even if they do not see?' (Yunus 10:42-43). And Allah created man equipped with both senses and intellect, so his responsibility is attached to both: 'And Allah brought you out from the wombs of your mothers knowing nothing, and He made for you hearing, sight, and hearts that you might be grateful' (An-Nahl 16:78). 'And He it is who produced for you hearing, sight, and hearts; little do you give thanks' (Al-Mu'minun 23:78). 'Then He proportioned him and breathed into him of His spirit and made for you hearing, sight, and hearts; little do you give thanks' (As-Sajdah 32:9). 'And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge; indeed the hearing, sight, and heart — all those will be questioned' (Al-Isra 17:36). It is worth noting in these verses that mentioning the hearts refers to the meaning of intellect, for it is the seat of all perception, to which the senses convey their perceptions, and it is knowledge of simple concepts. And the indication is clear that acquired sciences cannot be obtained except through intuitive sciences, both theoretical and necessary. The attainment of intuitive sciences occurs when their subjects and fields are conceived. The occurrence of these concepts is only due to the assistance of the senses regarding their particulars. Thus the five senses become the original cause for the occurrence of these sciences. Hearing and sight are the first senses in acquiring concepts and the most important. It is true that the verses distinguish man from animals by the intellect, for the animal has no heart despite possessing senses, but perhaps what some have claimed is not precise...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion from a Quranic perspective, focusing on the relationship between sensory perception and intellectual reasoning in acquiring knowledge. The author begins by citing the verse from Surah Al-Baqarah (2:258) about Prophet Ibrahim's debate with Nimrod, highlighting the inductive method: particular instances of giving life and controlling the universe lead to the universal conclusion of Allah's perfect power. The intellect is then brought in, which strategizes step-by-step debate to corner the opponent—this shows higher cognitive functions. Next, the author uses verses about 'Ad and Thamud (Al-Ankabut 29:38, As-Sajdah 32:26) to illustrate the historical method: observing ruins leads to understanding past punishments, combining sensory observation (seeing remnants) with intellectual inference. The passage then emphasizes the necessary connection (تلازم) between senses and intellect, quoting Yunus 10:42-43 to show that even if people have hearing and sight, without intellect they are like deaf and blind. Several verses (An-Nahl 16:78, Al-Mu'minun 23:78, As-Sajdah 32:9, Al-Isra 17:36) are cited to show that Allah gives humans hearing, sight, and hearts (intellect), and all are responsible. The author explains that 'hearts' (أفئدة) refers to the intellect, which is the seat of all perception; the senses convey their data to it, producing simple concepts. Finally, the author argues that acquired knowledge (علوم كسبية) is only possible through intuitive/knowledge (علوم بديهية), which themselves require concepts formed with the help of the senses. Thus the five senses are the original cause for knowledge, with hearing and sight being primary. The passage ends with a critical note that some claims about animal intellect may be imprecise, though the full discussion is cut off.

Nature of Intuition and Its Two-Fold Classification
Definition of intuition and its two-fold classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two key characteristics of intuition and distinguish between sensible and rational intuition using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Intuition yields true conclusions without the knower explaining how.

In this passage, the author defines intuition (hads) and highlights its peculiar feature: a person reaches correct conclusions without being able to explain the reasoning process. He also notes affective indicators like pleasure and certainty, and then presents a two-fold classification. This lesson will help you grasp these features and apply the classification to examples from Islamic disciplines such as kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic of intuition
Look for the statement that a person cannot explain the foundations or methods behind a correct conclusion. This inability to articulate the reasoning process is central to intuition.
2
Note the affective indicators
The passage mentions feelings of pleasure, comfort, and subjective certainty as signs that a conclusion is intuitive. These are not proofs but accompanying experiences.
3
Recognize the sudden assessment feature
Intuition often manifests as rapid assessment in unexpected situations, requiring decisive action. This speed is a hallmark of intuitive powers.
4
Understand the two-fold division
The author introduces two types: sensible intuition (sensory perception, e.g., seeing a color) and rational intuition (basis for demonstration, e.g., grasping a logical truth). Distinguish them by their source: senses vs. intellect.
5
Apply the classification to Islamic examples
For instance, the immediate sense of awe upon hearing the Quran is sensible intuition, while the insight that the Quran is inimitable (i'jaz) may be rational intuition. Practice classifying such cases.

ويبدو أن هذا الخلاف سبب مباشر في التداخل الذي يبرز أحيانًا بين الحدس والإلهام، فنجد أن ثمة من يستخدم هذا المصطلح مرادفا للمصطلح الآخر. وإن كان هذا لا يغير من حقيقة وجود الحدس بسمات ومظاهر خاصة. ولعل أبرز ما يستدعي التوقف في ظاهرة الحدس ما يؤكد عليه علماء النفس الحديث من أنه غدا بإمكان الإنسان الوصول إلى استنتاجات يثبت بعد ذلك صحتها، بيد أنه لا يستطيع شرح الأسس التي تقوم عليها هذه الاستنتاجات أو الطرائق التي سلكها في سبيلها. هذا مع أن الناس يختلفون في غيرها من القدرات. ومن المؤشرات الوجدانية على صحة الاستنتاج شعور المرء بالسرور والارتياح وكذلك اليقين على مستوى الذات. كما أنه من الملفت للنظر حقاً ما يلاحظ من سرعة تقدير لما يحتاج إليه الموقف المفاجئ من عمل حاسم، وذلك ما يعتقد أنه من أبرز المظاهر المميزة لذوي القوى الحدسية. أقسام الحدس يختلف الباحثون في تقسيم الحدس إلى فريقين فريق يرى أن الحدس لا يزيد على قسمين: الأول: إدراك حسي ويسمى حدسًا حسيا. الثاني: أساس للبرهنة والاستدلال، ويسمى الحدس العقلي.
It appears that this disagreement is a direct cause of the overlap that sometimes emerges between intuition and inspiration, for we find that some use this term as synonymous with the other term. Although this does not change the reality of intuition having distinctive features and manifestations. Perhaps the most noteworthy thing in the phenomenon of intuition is what modern psychologists emphasize: that it has become possible for a person to reach conclusions whose correctness is later proven, yet he cannot explain the foundations upon which these conclusions are based or the methods he followed in their pursuit. This, even though people differ in other abilities. Among the affective indicators of the correctness of the conclusion is the person's feeling of pleasure and comfort, as well as certainty at the subjective level. Also, truly striking is the observed rapid assessment of what an unexpected situation requires in terms of decisive action, and this is believed to be one of the most prominent distinguishing features of those with intuitive powers. Divisions of intuition: Researchers differ in dividing intuition into two groups: one group holds that intuition is no more than two divisions: first: sensory perception, called sensible intuition. Second: a basis for demonstration and reasoning, called rational intuition.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage defines intuition and discusses its nature. It begins by noting that there is overlap between intuition and inspiration, but insists that intuition has its own distinct features. The key characteristic highlighted is that a person can reach correct conclusions intuitively without being able to explain the reasoning process. This is a sign of intuition, along with emotional feelings of pleasure and certainty. The passage then presents the first of two classifications: a two-fold division into sensible intuition (direct sensory perception) and rational intuition (basis for demonstration). The author does not yet evaluate this classification; that comes later.

The Four-Fold Division of Intuition and Its Critique
Author presents four-fold division of intuition and critiques spiritual types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish reliable from unreliable types of intuition by applying the author's criterion of testability by reason and sense.

Core Idea
Not all intuitions are equal: only those testable by reason and sense are reliable.

Intuition is often invoked as a source of knowledge, but not all intuitions carry equal weight. In this passage, the author presents a four-fold classification of intuition and critiques two types—unveiling and philosophical-Sufi intuition—as unreliable because they may contradict revelation, sense, and reason. He then reaffirms the standard two-fold classification (empirical and rational) as the only rigorous basis for intuition as a source of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of intuition listed
Find the four categories: empirical, rational, unveiling, and philosophical/Sufi. Note the Arabic terms.
The author uses 'حدس تجريبي', 'حدس عقلي', 'حدس كشفي', 'حدس فلسفي أو صوفي'.
2
Understand the author's critique of the last two types
Why does the author reject unveiling and philosophical-Sufi intuition? They arise from special states, often contradict revelation, sense, and reason, and can be influenced by false beliefs.
The critique is based on epistemological and shar'i grounds, not mere opinion.
3
Identify the author's own position
The author adopts the first classification (empirical and rational) because it is rigorous and generalizable, being subject to the two primary sources: sense and reason.
This reflects a commitment to a testable, verifiable epistemology.
4
Extract the condition for accepting intuition
Intuitive thinking must be tested in light of rational concepts and sensory perceptions. It is not an independent source but a complementary tool when information is lacking.
This condition is crucial for avoiding errors in knowledge claims.

وفريق آخر يوصلها إلى أربعة أقسام على النحو التالي: الأول: الحدس التجريبي الثاني: الحدس العقلي الثالث: الحدس الكشفي الرابع: الحدس الفلسفي أو الصوفي (إدراك الإشراقيين). والحق أن التصنيف الأول هو المعتمد في هذه الدراسة لانضباطه وإمكان تعميمه مادام وأنه خاضع للمصدرين الأساسيين الحس والعقل). أما الحدسان الكشفي والفلسفي الصوفي فإنهما يصدران عن أحوال خاصة، تتفق وتتضارب أحيانًا كثيرة. وقد تأتي بما يخالف المصادر الرئيسة من وحي وحس وعقل، أو تتأثر في كشفها وإشراقها بمعتقداتها ولو كانت باطلة. ولذلك فالتفكير الحدسي ليس كافيًا وحده - بصرف النظر عن هذين النوعين – لأن يحتل مرتبته مستقلا في مصادر المعرفة وطرائقها، فلابد أن يختبر في ضوء مفهومات العقل ومدركات الحس. أهمية الحدس والحدس - وفقًا لهذا المفهوم - يحتل أهميةً تتجلى في إدراك بعض الحقائق الحسية والعقلية - تبعاً لنوعيته - وذلك عندما تنقصنا المعلومات اللازمة للحصول على المعرفة المطلوبة. وإذا كانت قلة المعلومات المتاحة دافعا هاما نحو إعمال التفكير الحدسي، فإن حل المشكلات التي تبرز بين الحين والآخر يتطلب تفكيراً حدسياً ليعوض تلك.
Another group extends it to four divisions as follows: first: empirical intuition; second: rational intuition; third: unveiling intuition; fourth: philosophical or Sufi intuition (the perception of the Illuminationists). The truth is that the first classification is the one adopted in this study for its rigor and generalizability, as long as it is subject to the two primary sources: sense and reason. As for the unveiling and philosophical-Sufi intuitions, they arise from special states, sometimes agreeing and often conflicting. They may produce things that contradict the primary sources of revelation, sense, and reason, or they may be influenced in their unveiling and illumination by their beliefs, even if false. Therefore, intuitive thinking alone is not sufficient – apart from these two types – to occupy an independent rank among the sources and methods of knowledge; it must be tested in light of rational concepts and sensory perceptions. The importance of intuition: intuition – according to this concept – holds an importance manifested in the perception of some sensible and rational truths – depending on its type – when we lack the necessary information to obtain the required knowledge. And if the scarcity of available information is an important motive for employing intuitive thinking, then solving problems that arise from time to time requires intuitive thinking to compensate for that.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the alternative four-fold classification of intuition: empirical, rational, unveiling (kashfi), and philosophical/Sufi. The author then rejects the third and fourth types, arguing that they are based on special states, often contradict revelation, sense, and reason, and are influenced by false beliefs. Therefore, the author adopts the first (two-fold) classification as more rigorous. The passage concludes by affirming the importance of intuition as a source of knowledge when information is lacking, especially for solving problems, but insists that intuition must always be tested by reason and sense.

Distinguishing Intuition and Inspiration
Author defines and distinguishes hads and ilham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish intuition (hads) from inspiration (ilham) based on temporal development, prediction, and mode of perception.

Core Idea
Intuition builds over time; inspiration strikes in a flash.

In this passage, the author explores two subsidiary knowledge sources: intuition and inspiration. He provides a precise distinction through an everyday example and highlights their commonality as inner immediate perception. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating claims based on intuition or inspiration in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining features of intuition (hads)
Look for the temporal, predictive, and anticipatory character. How does the author describe the example of trusting aversion after a period?
Intuition relies on accumulated subtle signals over time.
2
Identify the defining features of inspiration (ilham)
Note its suddenness, directness, and lack of logical premises. The author describes it as being 'cast into the heart as a ray of light.'
Inspiration is instantaneous and not built on prior analysis.
3
Compare their common ground
Both are inner, taste-based, and immediate. The passage mentions a connection some researchers see between them.
Despite differences, they share a non-rational, direct mode of perception.
4
Apply the distinction to the given example
The passage presents an example of trusting a feeling of aversion after a period. Determine which source it illustrates.
The example illustrates intuition because it involves prediction and time.
